Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Maximum Defect in Udhiyyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on maximum permissible defect

Learning Objective
State the multiple narrations from Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the maximum permissible cut from an udhiyyah, and explain which view is mufta bihi according to later authorities.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the maximum allowed cut from an udhiyyah is either one-third (strongest) or majority remains (Sahibayn), with later fatwa favoring one-third.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the maximum defect allowed in an udhiyyah (sacrificial animal). Imam Abu Hanifa's narrations vary between one-third, one-quarter, and one-third based on a hadith, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that as long as the majority of the animal remains, the sacrifice is valid. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals the methodology of Hanafi reasoning and the process of tarjih (preference) in later fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses a specific fiqhi question: what is the maximum amount of a limb (tail, ear, eye, or fat-tail) that may be cut from an udhiyyah without invalidating the sacrifice? This is a condition of validity.
2
State Abu Hanifa's narrations and their proofs
Three narrations from Abu Hanifa: (1) from al-Jami' al-Saghir: a third or less is valid, more invalid; proof: analogy to will where one-third is permissible without heirs' consent. (2) quarter: based on prayer rulings where quarter signals completeness. (3) third: based on the Prophet's hadith on wills: 'The third is much.'
Each narration has a different dalil: qiyas, qiyas in prayer, and hadith.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if the majority of the animal remains (i.e., more than half), it suffices. They rely on 'consideration of reality' (اعتبار الحقيقة) similar to prayer rulings where half or more is significant.
This is a more 'reality-based' approach focused on the overall health of the animal.
4
Determine the mufta bihi according to later authorities
Allamah Shami in Radd al-Muhtar states that one-third and below is 'little' and above is 'much', and this is the relied-upon fatwa. In Sukab al-Anhar, the majority-exists view is also mentioned but Shami's view from Sharh al-Kanz is considered correct.
Thus the operative ruling today: cutting a third or less does not invalidate; more does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفت الرواية عن ابي حنيفة في مقدار الاكثر ففى الجامع الصغير عنه وان قطع من الذنب او الاذن او العين او الالية الثلث او اقل اجزاه وان كان اكثر لم يجزه لان الثلث تنفذفيه الوصية من غير رضاء الورثة فاعتبر قليلا وفيما زاد لا تنفذ الابرضاهم فاعتبر كثيرا ويروى عنه الربع لانه يحكى حكاية الكمال على مامر فى الصلوة ويروى الثلث لقوله عليه السلام في حديث الوصية الثلث والثلث كثير . وقال ابو يوسف ومحمد اذا بقى الاكثر من النصف اجزاه اعتبار للحقيقة على ما تقدم في الصلوة وهو اختيار الفقيه ابي الليث وقال ابو يوسف اخبرت بقولى اباحنيفة فقال قولى هو قولك قيل هو رجوع منه الى قول ابى يوسف وقيل معناه قولى قريب من قولك وفى كون النصف مانعا روايتان عنهما كما في انكشاف العضو عن ابي يوسف. تنبیہ علامہ شامی نے ردالمختار ص ۲۰۶ ج ۵ پر اور اس سے کم کو کیل کہا ہے اور اس سے زائد کو کثیر ۔ اور پھر فر مایا علیہ الفتوى ۔ سکب الانہر ص ۴۴۱ ج ۲ پر اولاً بحوالہ حتمی کہا ہے کہ بقاء اکثر پر اکتفاء کیا جائے گا اور کہا ہے و علیہ الفتوی، اور پھر بحوالہ شرح الكنز لا بن السلطان کہا ہے الثلث و مادونه قليل و مازاد عليه كثير هو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى
And the narration from Abu Hanifa regarding the maximum amount is differed: in al-Jami' al-Saghir from him, if he cuts from the tail, ear, eye, or fat-tail a third or less, it suffices, and if more, it does not suffice, because a third is executed in the will without the consent of the heirs, so it is considered little, and what exceeds is not executed except with their consent, so it is considered much. And it is narrated from him: a quarter, because it reports the state of perfection as passed in Salat. And it is narrated: a third, because of his saying peace be upon him in the hadith of the will: 'The third, and the third is much.' And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: if the majority remains from half, it suffices, considering the reality as passed in Salat, and this is the choice of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. And Abu Yusuf said: I informed Abu Hanifa of my saying, and he said: 'My saying is your saying.' It is said that this is a retraction from him to the saying of Abu Yusuf, and it is said its meaning is: my saying is close to your saying. And regarding half being a preventer, there are two narrations from them, as in the uncovering of the limb from Abu Yusuf. Note: Allamah Shami said in Radd al-Muhtar, vol. 5, p. 206: one-third and less is little, and what exceeds is much. Then he said: upon it is the fatwa. In Sukab al-Anhar, vol. 2, p. 441, first by citation of Hatem, he said: sufficiency is upon the existence of the majority, and he said: upon it is the fatwa. And then by citation of Sharh al-Kanz li-Ibn al-Sultan, he said: one-third and below is little, and what exceeds is much, and it is the correct, and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute examining the maximum permissible defect in an udhiyyah. Three narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa are presented: first, from al-Jami' al-Saghir, that the cut of a third or less of a limb is excusable, but more invalidates; second, a narration allowing a quarter, based on its being a sign of completeness in prayer rulings; third, a narration setting the limit at a third, citing the Prophet's hadith on wills where a third is considered 'much'. The Sahibayn (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that if the majority of the animal remains (i.e., more than half), it suffices, focusing on the actual health of the animal. Abu Yusuf reports that Abu Hanifa appeared to agree with his view, though scholars interpret this as either a retraction or a statement of closeness. Two narrations from the Sahibayn treat half as a preventer, as discussed in the chapter on prayer conditions. The note from Allamah Shami indicates that later fatwa practice has varied: some rely on the existence of the majority, while others adopt the rule that a third or less is little and more is much, which is considered correct and the basis for fatwa.

Key Terms
الأكثر (al-akthar): The maximum permissible amount of a limb that can be cut from the udhiyyah without invalidating it.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third; in this context, the median accepted limit for defect.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): Will; used as analogy for the permissibility of one-third without heirs' consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Allamah Shami in Radd al-Muhtar, what is the correct fatwa regarding the maximum cut from an udhiyyah?
A) If the majority of the animal remains, it is valid.
B) One-third and below is little; above is much and invalidates.
C) Half is the dividing line.
D) Any cut less than a quarter is valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi view on maximum udhiyyah defect is that cuts of one-third or less are permissible; anything more invalidates the sacrifice.

Method to Measure Eye Impairment in Udhiyyah
Practical method to measure blindness in a sacrificial animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method for measuring vision loss in a sheep's eye and apply it to quantify the defect as one-third, half, etc.

Core Idea
Blindness is objectively quantified by comparing the seeing distances of the healthy and defective eyes.

In the Hanafi school, the validity of an udhiyah depends on the extent of defects like blindness. This passage provides a precise, reproducible method to measure how much vision is lost in a sheep's eye. Instead of subjective guesswork, the jurists devised a practical test using the animal's natural behavior to ensure fairness and consistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose
Recognize that this method is used to determine whether the blindness in a sheep's eye exceeds the permissible limit (usually one-third) for udhiyyah.
2
Identify the preparatory step
The sheep must be deprived of grazing for a day or two so it becomes eager for fodder, ensuring it will seek the feed during the test.
3
Test the defective eye
Tie the defective eye, then slowly bring fodder closer until the sheep sees it. Mark that spot.
4
Test the healthy eye
Tie the healthy eye, repeat the process, and mark the distance at which the sheep sees the fodder.
5
Calculate the fraction
Measure the difference between the two distances. If the difference is one-third of the healthy eye's distance, then one-third of vision is lost; if half, then half is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معرفة المقدار فى غير العين ميسر وفى العين قالو اتشد العين المعيبة بعد ان لا تعتلف الشاة يوما اويومين ثم يقرب العلف اليها قليلا قليلا فاذا راته من موضع اعلم على ذلك المكان ثم تشد عينها الصحيحة وقرب اليها العلف قليلا قليلا حتى اذا راته من مكان اعلم اليه ثم ينظر الى تفاوت ما بينهما فان كان ثلثا فالذاهب الثلث وان كان نصفا فالنصف
Then, knowledge of the measure in other than the eye is easy, and regarding the eye, they said: tie the defective eye after the sheep does not graze for a day or two, then bring the fodder close to it little by little, and when it sees it from a place, mark that place. Then tie its healthy eye and bring the fodder close to it little by little until when it sees it from a place, mark that place. Then look at the difference between them: if it is a third, then the lost is a third; if it is half, then half.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a practical ruling detailing how to measure the extent of blindness in a sheep's eye for the purpose of udhiyyah validity. The method involves temporarily depriving the sheep of food for a day or two, then testing each eye separately by observing from what distance it sees fodder. The difference between the distance for the defective eye and the healthy eye indicates the fraction of vision lost. If the difference is one-third, then one-third of the vision is lost; if half, then half. This is important because the ruling on udhiyyah depends on whether the defect (like blindness) exceeds the permissible maximum (as discussed in the previous lesson).

Key Terms
العين المعيبة (al-'ayn al-mu'ayyaba): The defective or blind eye of the animal being tested.
العلف (al-'ulf): Fodder or feed used to attract the sheep during the test.
التفاوت (al-tafawut): The difference in distance between the two tests, used to calculate the fraction of vision loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After tying the sheep's defective eye, what is the first step in measuring vision loss?
A) Immediately bring fodder close
B) Leave the sheep without grazing for a day or two
C) Mark the distance from which the sound eye sees
D) Compare both eyes simultaneously

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for measuring blindness in a sheep's eye uses fasting and distance comparison to produce an objective fraction of vision loss.

Sacrifice of Hornless and Broken-Horned Animals
Ruling on hornless and broken-horn animals for sacrifice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sacrificing hornless and broken-horned animals and identify the condition that invalidates sacrifice.

Core Idea
Only a horn broken from the root invalidates sacrifice; missing or surface-broken horns are acceptable.

Sacrificing an animal on Eid al-Adha is a cherished act of worship. But what if the animal lacks horns or has a broken horn? The Hanafi school provides clear guidance based on the purpose of the horn. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the matn of al-Quduri and its later commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is sacrificing an animal (udhiyyah), an act of worship. In the Hanafi school, udhiyyah is sunnah mu'akkadah.
Not mentioned explicitly in the passage but important context.
2
Determine the hukm level for horn condition
The passage rules that sacrificing a hornless animal or one with a surface break is valid (sahih).
3
Analyze the rationale
The reason given is that no essential purpose (maqsud) is attached to the horn—the meat and health of the animal are unaffected.
4
Identify the invalidating condition
If the horn is broken from the root and the break reaches the marrow, the sacrifice becomes invalid because the animal is then defective.
This distinction is from later Hanafi commentary (Shami).
5
Note the authority level
The base ruling is from Quduri's matn; the clarification about root break is from later works like Rad al-Mukhtar (Shami).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جس کے بالکل سینگ نہ ہوں یا جڑ سے نہ ٹوٹا تو قربانی درست ہے 
قال ويجوز ان يضحى بالجماء وهى التى لا قرن لها لان القرن لا يتعلق به مقصود وكذا مكسورة القرن لما قلنا 
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جماء کی قربانی جائز ہے اور یہ وہ ہے جس کے سینگ نہ ہوں اس لئے کہ سینگ اس کے ساتھ مقصود متعلق نہیں ہے۔ اور ایسے ہی سینگ ٹوٹی ہوئی اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تشریح سینگ بالکل نہ ہوں یا ٹوٹ جانا جو از قربانی کے لئے مانع نہیں ۔ ہاں اگر سینگ جڑ سے اکھڑ جائے کہ ٹوٹن کا اثر گودے تک پہنچ جائے تو اس کی قربانی جائز نہ ہوگی ۔ شامی ص ۲۰۵ ج ۵-
If it has no horns at all or the horn is not broken from the root, then sacrifice is valid. He (al-Quduri) said: And it is permissible to sacrifice a jamma', which is one that has no horn, because no purpose is attached to the horn. Similarly, a broken-horn [animal] is permissible for the reason we have mentioned. Translation: Imam Quduri said: And sacrifice of a jamma' is permissible, and it is one that has no horn because no purpose is attached to the horn. And similarly a broken-horned animal is permissible due to the reason we have stated. Explanation: Having no horns at all or broken horn is not a barrier for sacrifice. Yes, if the horn is uprooted from the root such that the effect of the break reaches the marrow, then its sacrifice will not be permissible. (Shami vol.5 p.205)
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Quduri with commentary. The key point: hornlessness (jamma') or a simple horn break does not make an animal defective for sacrifice because horns serve no essential purpose for the meat. However, if the horn is broken from the root to expose the marrow, that harms the animal and renders it unsuitable. This distinction is from later Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
جماء (jammā'): A female animal without horns from birth or due to breakage.
اضحية (uḍḥiyyah): The sacrificial animal offered on Eid al-Adha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of horn break invalidates a sacrificial animal according to this passage?
A) Any break
B) Break from the root exposing the marrow
C) Break that makes the horn shorter than one-third
D) Break that occurs after purchase

Key Takeaway
Only a horn broken from the root to the marrow invalidates a sacrificial animal; hornlessness and superficial breaks are acceptable.

Ruling on Castrated Animals
Ruling with hadith proof on castrated animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sacrificing castrated animals, cite the hadith proof, and explain why castration is not a defect.

Core Idea
Sacrifice of castrated animals is not only valid but recommended—the Prophet himself sacrificed them.

This lesson addresses a common question: are castrated animals suitable for udhiyah? The Hanafi school answers with a clear ruling supported by a hadith of the Prophet. We will examine the proof and the reasoning behind the permissibility, and understand why this position differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sacrificing a castrated animal. The ruling is permissible and even recommended.
The animal is called 'khasī' or 'mawjū''.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a sahih hadith that the Prophet sacrificed two castrated rams.
Hadith: 'The Prophet sacrificed two rams that were white and black (spotted) and castrated.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action demonstrates permissibility and virtue. Since the Prophet chooses the best for sacrifice, castration does not detract.
Implies that castration is not a defect.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools consider castration a major defect that invalidates the sacrifice.
Their basis: perfection required, castration removes a body part.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon due to the authentic hadith overriding general principles.
Mufta bihi: permissible and preferable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خصی کی قربانی کا حکم
والخصى لان لحمها اطيب وقد صح ان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ضحى بكبشين املحين موجوئين
ترجمہ اور خصی کی قربانی جائز ہے۔ اس لئے کہ اس کا گوشت لذیذ ہوتا ہے اور یہ بات درجہ صحت کو پہنچی ہوئی ہے کہ نبی کریم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے دو مینڈھوں کی قربانی کی جو سیاہ وسفید تھے ( چتکبرے) جو دونوں خصی تھے ، اس کی تفسیر ظاہر ہے۔
Ruling on sacrificing a castrated animal. And the castrated [is permissible] because its meat is more delicious, and it is authentically reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) sacrificed two rams that were white and black (spotted) which were both castrated. Explanation is clear.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The Hanafi position: castrated animals are not only permissible but recommended because the meat is better tasting. The proof is a hadith that the Prophet himself sacrificed castrated rams, establishing practice.

Key Terms
خصي (khaṣī): A castrated male animal.
موجوء (mawjū'): Castrated; the term used in the hadith for the rams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the permissibility of sacrificing a castrated animal according to this passage?
A) The meat is tastier
B) The Prophet sacrificed castrated rams
C) It is easier to handle
D) It is less expensive

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's own practice establishes that castrated animals are not only permissible but recommended for sacrifice.

Ruling on Sacrificing a Mad Animal (Thawlā')
Ruling on sacrifice of mad animals with condition of grazing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sacrificing a mad animal (thawlā') and identify the condition that determines its validity.

Core Idea
A mad animal (thawlā') is valid for sacrifice as long as it can graze normally—the madness itself is irrelevant if the animal feeds.

You might think a mad animal can never be used for sacrifice. But in Hanafi fiqh, the key is not the madness itself but whether the animal can still graze. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Quduri's matn and clarifies that the sacrifice is valid if the animal eats grass, because the purpose of sacrifice (obtaining meat) is not undermined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal and its condition
The passage discusses a 'thawlā'' animal, a mad female that runs aimlessly. Recognize that the term refers specifically to an animal with erratic behavior.
The madness is a state, not a legal category by itself.
2
State the default ruling
The default ruling is that sacrificing such an animal is permissible (sahih).
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Locate the central condition
The condition that changes the ruling is whether the animal can graze (eat grass) normally. This is taken from the phrase "اذا كانت تعتلف" (if it eats grass).
Grazing indicates the animal can still fulfill its purpose.
4
Apply the condition
If it grazes, the sacrifice is valid; if it does not graze, it is invalid. The ruling hinges on this functional test.
The condition is about ability, not whether the animal is completely healthy.
5
Understand the rationale
The purpose of sacrifice is to obtain meat. Grazing is essential for the animal to be healthy and provide acceptable meat. Madness that does not prevent grazing does not interfere with this purpose.
This rationale is given in the text: "لانه لا يخل بالمقصود" (because it does not interfere with the purpose).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجنون جانور کی قربانی کا حکم
والثولاء وهى المجنونة وقيل هذا اذا كانت تعتلف لانه لا يخل بالمقصود اما اذا كانت لا تعتلف لا تجزيه
ترجمہ اور تولا یعنی مجنونہ کی قربانی جائز ہے اور کہا گیا کہ یہ جب ہے جب کہ وہ گھاس کھاتی ہو۔۔ اس لئے کہ مقصود کے اندر مخل نہیں ہے بہر حال جب کہ وہ گھاس نہ چرتی ہو تو جائز نہیں ہے۔
تشریح حیوانات تو غیر ذوی العقول ہی ہوتے ہیں تو یہاں جنون سے مراد خواہ مخواہ ادھرادھ بلاگا پھرتا ہے تو اگر گھاس کھاتی ہے تو اس کی قربانی جائز ہے ورنہ نہیں۔
Ruling on sacrificing a mad animal. And al-thawla' is the mad one. It is said: This is when it eats grass, because it does not interfere with the purpose. But if it does not eat grass, it is not acceptable. Translation: And thawla meaning a mad female is permissible for sacrifice, and it has been said that this is when it eats grass, because it does not interfere with the purpose. However, when it does not graze, it is not permissible. Explanation: Animals are non-rational beings, so here madness means it runs around aimlessly. If it eats grass, its sacrifice is permissible; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on sacrificing mad animals. The term 'thawla'' refers to an animal with erratic behavior. The key condition: the madness does not invalidate the animal unless it prevents the animal from grazing normally. If it still eats grass, the purpose of sacrifice (obtaining meat) is fulfilled.

Key Terms
ثولاء (thawlā'): A female animal with madness, running aimlessly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a mad animal (thawlā') not permissible for sacrifice?
A) When it has seizures
B) When it does not eat grass
C) When it is female
D) When it is old

Key Takeaway
A mad animal is valid for sacrifice if it can still graze, because the madness does not hinder the purpose of obtaining meat.

Ruling on Sacrifice of Mangy Animals: Fatness as the Criterion
Ruling on sacrifice of mangy animals based on fatness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mangy animals for sacrifice and apply the condition of fatness to determine validity.

Core Idea
Mange invalidates sacrifice only if it reaches the meat; a fat mangy animal is valid.

This passage from Quduri's matn addresses the sacrifice of mangy animals. The key is not the mere presence of mange, but whether it has caused the animal to become thin. If the animal remains fat, the defect is only skin-deep and the sacrifice is valid. If it has wasted away, the defect has affected the meat and the sacrifice is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sacrifice (udhiyah) of a mangy animal. The ruling depends on the animal's condition.
Hanafi school considers udhiyah a wajib act for those who can afford it.
2
Determine the two conditions
The passage presents two scenarios: the animal is either fat (سمينة) or lean (مهزولة).
Fatness here refers to the animal's body not having thinned due to mange.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
If the animal is fat, the mange is confined to the skin (في الجلد) and does not affect the meat. If it is lean, the mange has penetrated the meat (في اللحم) and caused deficiency.
The reasoning is based on observable physical condition.
4
Apply the ruling
Fat mangy animal → sacrifice permissible (جائز). Lean mangy animal → sacrifice not permissible (لاتجوز).
Permissible here means the sacrifice is valid (sahih).
5
Note the practical consequence
The criterion is weight loss due to mange, not the mere presence of mange. A mangy animal that remains fat is acceptable.
This distinguishes Hanafi from schools that consider mange an absolute defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجرباء ان كانت سمينة جاز لان الجرب فى الجلد ولا نقصان فى اللحم وان كانت مهزولة لاتجوز لان الجرب في اللحم فانتقص
ترجمہ اور جرباء ( خارش والا ) اگر موٹا ہے تو جائز ہے اس لئے کہ خارش چمڑے میں ہے اور گوشت میں کوئی نقصان نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر جزبہ ذلیل ہو جائے تو جائز نہیں۔ اسلئے کہ خارش گوشت میں سے پس نقصان پیدا ہو گیا۔
تشریح اور اگر خارش ہے تو دو صورتیں ہیں۔ اس خارش کی وجہ سے بدن ڈبل ہوا ہے یا نہیں اگر ہوا ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ خارش گوشت تک ہے تو جائز نہیں ، اور اگر بدن ڈبل انہیں ہوا تو معلوم ہوگا کہ خارش کھال میں ہے۔ لہذا اس کی قربانی جائز ہے۔
And al-jarba' (mangy), if it is fat, it is permissible because the mange is in the skin and there is no deficiency in the meat. If it is lean, it is not permissible because the mange is in the meat, so it has decreased. Translation: And jarba' (mangy), if it is fat, it is permissible because the mange is in the skin and there is no deficiency in the meat. And if it becomes lean, it is not permissible because the mange has affected the meat, so deficiency has occurred. Explanation: And if there is mange, there are two cases: due to the mange, the body has become thin or not. If it has become thin, we know that the mange has reached the meat, so it is not permissible. If the body has not become thin, we know that the mange is in the skin; therefore its sacrifice is permissible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on mangy animals. The key factor is whether the mange has caused the animal to lose weight. If the animal is still fat, the mange is only skin-deep and does not affect the meat. If the animal has become lean, the mange has penetrated the flesh, making the animal defective for sacrifice.

Key Terms
جرباء (jarbā'): A mangy animal with skin disease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a mangy animal valid for sacrifice?
A) When it has mange everywhere
B) When it is still fat
C) When it is a goat
D) When it is young

Key Takeaway
The validity of a mangy sacrificial animal depends on whether the mange has caused it to become thin; if fat, it is valid.

Rulings on Toothless and Earless Animals for Sacrifice
Rulings on toothless and earless animals with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling for toothless and earless animals in sacrifice, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf from Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A toothless animal is valid if it can still graze; an earless animal is invalid by birth, by analogy with cut ears.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh literature addresses two defects in sacrificial animals: toothlessness and earlessness. While the general principle is that such defects may invalidate the sacrifice, the Hanafi school provides nuanced rulings, especially regarding toothlessness, where there is an internal dispute among the imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defects and their default rulings
The passage discusses al-hatma' (toothless) and al-sakka' (earless). Note that for toothlessness there is an ikhtilaf, while for earlessness the ruling is clear: invalid.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on toothlessness
Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one requiring a majority of teeth, the other focusing on the animal's ability to graze. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The first narration is stricter; the second is more lenient.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (operative) ruling
The commentary indicates that the ability to graze is the relied-upon criterion. Thus, a toothless animal that can still graze is valid.
4
Understand the ruling for earlessness by analogy
The passage uses qiyas: if a cut ear (majority missing) invalidates, then complete absence (by birth) is even more so invalid. Small ears are not a defect.
5
Apply the rulings to practice
For a toothless but grazing-capable animal, it is valid. For an earless-by-birth animal, it is invalid except if ears are small.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اما الهتماء وهي التي لا اسنان لها فعن ابي يوسف انه يعتبر فى الاسنان الكثرة والقلة وعنه ان بقى ما يمكن الاعتلاف به اجزاء لحصول المقصود والسكاء وهي التي لا اذن لها خلقة لا تجوز ان كان هذا لان مقطوع اكثر الاذن اذا كان لا يجوز فعديم الاذن اولى
ترجمہ اور بہر حال ہتما ء اور یہ وہ ہے جس کے دانت نہ ہوں تو ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ وہ دانتوں میں کثرت وقلت کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں جن سے چہر ناممکن ہو تو جائز ہے مقصود کے حاصل ہونے کی وجہ سے اور سگا اور یہ وہ ہے جس کے پیدائشی کان نہ ہوں جائز نہیں ہے تو جس کا کان نہ ہو وہ بدرجہ اولی جائز نہ ہوگا۔
تشریح اور جس کے دانت نہ ہوں اس کی قربانی جائز نہیں ہے لیکن اس میں دو قول ہیں:
(1) اکثر کا باقی رہنا کافی ہے۔
گھاس کھانا کافی ہے اور اس کی قربانی جائز ہے۔

And as for al-hatma' (toothless), it is one that has no teeth. From Abu Yusuf: He considers the abundance or scarcity of teeth. And from him [another narration]: If enough remains to allow grazing, it is acceptable because the purpose is achieved. And al-sakka' (earless) is one that has no ears by nature; it is not permissible. This is because if the majority of the ear is cut off, it is not permissible, so when ears are completely absent, it is even more so not permissible. Explanation: And if it has no teeth, its sacrifice is not permissible, but there are two opinions: (1) Majority remaining is sufficient. (2) If it can eat grass, it is sufficient and its sacrifice is permissible. And if it is born without ears, its sacrifice is not permissible because if the majority of the ear being cut prevents sacrifice, then complete absence is even more so. However, if the ears are small, its sacrifice is permissible.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf from Abu Yusuf on toothlessness. The first opinion requires a minimum number of teeth; the second allows sacrifice if the animal can still graze. The relied-upon position is the ability to graze. For earlessness, the ruling is that natural absence of ears is a defect by analogy with cut ears. Small ears are not a defect.

Key Terms
هتماء (hatmā'): A female animal without teeth or with insufficient teeth.
سكاء (sakkā'): An animal born without ears.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If it has more than half its teeth
B) If it can still graze normally
C) If it is young
D) If it is a sheep

Key Takeaway
A toothless animal is valid for sacrifice if it can graze; an animal born without ears is invalid by analogy with a cut ear.

Post-Purchase Defects: Rich vs Poor in Udhiya
Ruling on animal becoming defective after purchase for rich and poor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on post-purchase defects in sacrificial animals, distinguishing between rich and poor based on the source of obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation source determines replacement: rich bound by Sharī‘ah, poor bound by purchase.

This passage from al-Hidayah concludes the discussion on defects in sacrificial animals by addressing a common scenario: the animal becomes defective after purchase. The ruling hinges on the obligee's wealth status, because the obligation itself arises from different sources for the rich and the poor. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying the law in everyday practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes an animal purchased sound (salima) that later develops a defect preventing sacrifice (māni‘). Note that this is a post-purchase defect, not a pre-existing one.
Pre-existing defects were covered earlier; here the defect arises after ownership.
2
Classify the buyer as rich or poor
Determine whether the buyer is rich (ghanī) or poor (faqīr). The Hanafi criteria for wealth in udhiya is possession of nisab (excess wealth beyond basic needs).
This classification affects the nature of obligation.
3
Apply the rule based on obligation source
For the rich, the obligation (wujūb) is from the Shari‘ah itself, not contingent on purchase. Hence the animal does not become specified (ta‘ayyun) for the obligation; he must replace it. For the poor, obligation arises only from the purchase made with intention of sacrifice, so the animal becomes specified and suffices even if defective.
The rich's obligation is independent; the poor's is tied to purchase.
4
Note the compensation rule
The poor is not required to compensate the deficiency (ḍamān al-nuqṣān) in the value of the defective animal, analogous to the nisab of zakat.
This parallels zakat where a defective animal in the nisab does not require compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذى ذكرنا اذا كانت هذه العيوب قائمة وقت الشراء ولو اشتراها سليمة ثم تعيبت بعيب مانع ان كان غنيا عليه غيرها وان كان فقيرا تجزيه هذا لان الوجوب على الغنى بالشرع ابتداء لا بالشراء فلم تتعين به وعلى الفقير بشرائه بنية الاضحية فتعينت ولا يجب عليه ضمان نقصانه کمافی نصاب الزكوة
ترجمہ اور یہ تفصیل جو ہم نے ذکر کی ہے جب ہے جب کہ یہ عیوب خریدنے کے وقت موجود ہوں اور اگر اس کو صحیح سالم خریدا پھر وہ ایسے عیب سے عیب دار ہوگئی جو قربانی سے مانع ہے تو اگر وہ مالدار ہے تو اس پر اس کا غیر واجب اور اگر وہ فقیر ہے تو اس کو یہی کفایت کرے گی اس لئے کہ مالدار پر شروع ہی سے وجوب ہے شریعت کی وجہ سے نہ کہ خریدنے کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہی بکری وجوب کے لئے متعین نہیں ہے اور فقیر پر ( وجوب ) قربانی کی نیت سے خریدنے کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہی بکری تعین ہو گئی۔ اور فقیر پر اس کے نقصان کا ضمان واجب
And this that we have mentioned is when these defects exist at the time of purchase. If he bought it sound and then it became defective with a preventing defect, then if he is rich, another animal is required from him; if he is poor, this same [animal] suffices him. This is because the obligation upon the rich is from the Shari'ah initially, not due to purchase, so it is not specified by it. And upon the poor, [the obligation] is due to his purchase with the intention of sacrifice, so it becomes specified. And he is not obligated to compensate the deficiency, as in the nisab of zakat.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on defects by addressing the scenario when an animal becomes defective after purchase. The distinction is based on the nature of obligation: a rich person is obligated by Shari'ah (having nisab), so the animal he bought does not become incumbent; he must replace it if defective. A poor person's obligation arises only from his purchase with intention; thus, if the animal becomes defective, he may still sacrifice it and need not replace it. This parallels zakat rules.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujūb): Obligation; here the obligation to sacrifice.
تعين (ta'ayyun): Specification; the animal becomes determined for the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a rich person do if his sacrificial animal becomes defective after purchase?
A) Sacrifice it anyway
B) Buy another animal
C) Give it in charity
D) Compensate the difference

Key Takeaway
The source of obligation – Shari'ah for the rich, purchase intention for the poor – determines whether a post-purchase defect requires replacement or is excused.

Sacrifice of Toothless and Earless Animals: Hanafi Rulings
States rulings for hatma' and sakka' with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for sacrificing a toothless animal (hatma') and an earless animal (sakka'), and explain how the intra-Hanafi dispute is resolved.

Core Idea
A toothless animal suffices if it can graze; an animal born without ears is always invalid.

Sacrificial animals must be free from major defects. This passage examines two specific defects: total or near-total tooth loss (hatma') and congenital lack of ears (sakka'). The Hanafi school, through narrations from Imam Abu Yusuf, provides nuanced guidance on the hatma', while the sakka' is clearly prohibited. Understanding these rulings helps ensure your udhiyah meets the requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defects
The passage defines hatma' (toothless) and sakka' (born without ears). Note the specific definitions.
Hatma' refers to absence of all or most teeth; sakka' means total congenital absence of ears.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on hatma'
Two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one considers quantity of teeth, the other focuses on ability to graze. Understand the dalil for each.
The second narration (ability to graze) is the mufta bihi.
3
Derive the ruling for hatma'
Apply the preferred narration: if the animal can graze despite few teeth, the sacrifice is valid. Otherwise, it is invalid.
4
Derive the ruling for sakka'
The sakka' is invalid by qiyas: since cutting off most of the ear invalidates, total congenital absence is even more severe.
Small ears (present but undersized) are acceptable.
5
State the practical takeaway
Ensure sacrificial animals can eat normally and have at least partially formed ears.
These are two of several defects to check before sacrifice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(1) اکثر کا باقی رہنا کافی ہے۔

As for the hatma'—which is the one with no teeth—from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he considers abundance and paucity in teeth, and from him [another narration] that if what remains is sufficient for grazing, it suffices due to attainment of the purpose. And as-sakka'—which is the one born without ears—is not permissible, because if the majority of the ear when cut off is not permissible, then the one devoid [of ears] is even more so.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-statement on two specific defects in sacrificial animals: toothlessness (hatma') and being born without ears (sakka'). The commentator explains the Hanafi position, beginning with a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the hatma'. Abu Yusuf has two narrations: one that considers the quantity of remaining teeth, and another that considers whether the animal can still graze (i.e., if enough teeth remain for eating grass). The relied-upon position is that if the animal can graze, it is acceptable. For the sakka', the ruling is clear: the sacrifice is not valid. This is derived by analogy (qiyas jaliy) from the case where most of the ear is cut off, which is also invalid. The commentary adds that if the ears are small but present, the sacrifice is valid. The source for these rulings is the Hanafi school, with the weight being from the transmitted narrations of Abu Yusuf, which are part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الهتماء (al-hatma'): An animal with no teeth or very few teeth.
السكاء (as-sakka'): An animal born without ears.

Key Takeaway
For hatma', validity hinges on ability to graze; for sakka', the sacrifice is always invalid due to qiyas.

Ruling When a Sacrificial Animal Becomes Defective After Purchase: Rich vs. Poor
Explains the ruling when a sacrificial animal develops a defect after purchase, distinguishing between rich and poor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a sacrificial animal develops a defect after purchase, distinguishing between a wealthy and a poor owner, and explain the underlying principle of obligation and specification.

Core Idea
For the wealthy, sacrifice is a Shari'ah obligation; for the poor, it becomes specific by purchase, so defect affects each differently.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in the fiqh of sacrifice (udhiya). The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction between a wealthy person (ghani) and a poor person (faqir) when a purchased sacrificial animal becomes defective after the transaction. The difference hinges on the source of the obligation: for the wealthy, it is from the Shari'ah; for the poor, it arises from the intention to sacrifice, making the animal specified. Understanding this distinction clarifies why the ruling changes and reflects the precise legal reasoning of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the defect
The passage addresses two scenarios: defects existing at purchase (covered earlier) and defects occurring after purchase. Focus on the latter: animal sound at purchase, then becomes defective with a preventing defect.
2
Determine the wealth status
Classify the owner as ghani (wealthy, owner of nisab) or faqir (poor, not owner of nisab). The ruling differs based on this.
3
State the ruling for each
Wealthy: another animal is wajib. Poor: the defective animal suffices (tujzihi).
4
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The wealthy's obligation is from Shari'ah (due to nisab), so it is not tied to a specific animal; the poor's obligation is created by his purchase with intention of sacrifice, so it becomes specified (ta'ayyun). Therefore, no replacement is needed for the poor.
5
Apply the principle to other cases (death, loss, theft)
If the animal dies, wealthy must replace; poor does nothing. If lost and replaced, then found during sacrifice days: wealthy slaughters one; poor slaughters both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذى ذكرنا اذا كانت هذه العيوب قائمة وقت الشراء ولو اشتراها سليمة ثم تعيبت بعيب مانع ان كان غنيا عليه غيرها وان كان فقيرا تجزيه هذا لان الوجوب على الغنى بالشرع ابتداء لا بالشراء فلم تتعين به وعلى الفقير بشرائه بنية الاضحية فتعينت ولا يجب عليه ضمان نقصانه كما فى نصاب الزكوة
ترجمہ اور یہ تفصیل جو ہم نے ذکر کی ہے جب ہے جب کہ یہ عیوب خریدنے کے وقت موجود ہوں اور اگر اس کو صحیح سالم خریدا پھر وہ ایسے عیب سے عیب دار ہوگئی جو قربانی سے مانع ہے تو اگر وہ مالدار ہے تو اس پر اس کا غیر واجب اور اگر وہ فقیر ہے تو اس کو یہی کفایت کرے گی اس لئے کہ مالدار پر شروع ہی سے وجوب ہے شریعت کی وجہ سے نہ کہ خریدنے کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہی بکری وجوب کے لئے متعین نہیں ہے اور فقیر پر ( وجوب ) قربانی کی نیت سے خریدنے کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہی بکری تعین ہو گئی۔ اور فقیر پر اس کے نقصان کا ضمان واجب نہیں ہے جیسا کہ زکوۃ کے نصاب میں ۔
تشریح فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تفصیل اس وقت ہے جب کہ خریدنے کے وقت یہ عیوب موجود ہوں اور صحیح سالم خریدی اور پھر ایسا عیب پیدا ہو گیا کہ جس کی وجہ سے قربانی اس کی جائز نہیں رہی تو اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر وہ مالدار ہے تو اس پر دوسرا جانور خریدنا ضروری ہے۔ اور اگر فقیر ہے تو اسی کو قربان کر دے۔
وعن هذا الاصل قالوا اذا ماتت المشتراة للتضحية على الموسر مكانها اخرى ولا شئى على الفقير ولو ضلت او سرقت فاشترى اخرى ثم ظهرت الاولى فى ايام النحر على الموسر ذبح احدهما وعلى الفقير ذبحهما
ترجمہ اور اسی قاعدہ کے مطابق فقہاء نے فرمایا کہ جب کہ قربانی کے لئے خریدا ہوا جانور مر جائے تو مالدار پر اسی کی جگہ دوسرا ( خریدنا واجب ہے ) اور فقیر پر کچھ نہیں ہے اور اگر وہ گم ہو گیا یا چرایا گیا پھر اس نے دوسرا خرید لیا پھر ایام نحر میں پہلا مل گیا تو مالدار پر ان میں سے ایک کا ذبح واجب ہے اور فقیر پر ان دونوں کا ذبح کرنا واجب ہے۔
And this which we mentioned is when these defects exist at the time of purchase. But if he bought it sound and then it became defective with a preventing defect, then if he is wealthy, another is incumbent upon him; and if he is poor, this same one suffices him. This is because the obligation upon the wealthy is from the Shari'ah initially, not due to purchase, so it is not specified by it. And upon the poor, [the obligation] is by his purchase with the intention of sacrifice, so it is specified. And he is not liable for its deficiency, as in the nisab of zakat. And based on this principle, they said: if the animal purchased for sacrifice dies, then upon the wealthy, another in its place, and nothing upon the poor. And if it is lost or stolen and he bought another, then the first appears during the days of sacrifice, then upon the wealthy, he slaughters one of them, and upon the poor, he slaughters both.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling when a sacrificial animal develops a defect after purchase, distinguishing between a wealthy person (ghani) and a poor person (faqir). The key principle is that for the wealthy, the obligation of sacrifice is imposed by the Shari'ah from the outset (based on ownership of nisab), not by the act of purchasing an animal. Therefore, if the animal becomes defective, the obligation is not tied to that specific animal, and he must sacrifice another. For the poor person, the obligation arises from his purchase with the intention of sacrifice, so the animal becomes specified (ta'ayyun). Hence, even if it becomes defective, it suffices, and he is not required to compensate for the loss. The ruling is proven by analogy (qiyas) with zakat: if some of the nisab is destroyed before paying zakat, only the remaining amount is subject to zakat. The same principle applies if the animal dies, is lost, or stolen. If it dies: the wealthy must buy another, the poor does nothing. If lost and another bought, then the original is found during the days of sacrifice: the wealthy slaughters one, the poor must slaughter both because each purchase created an obligation for him.

Key Terms
الغني (al-ghani): A person who owns the nisab (minimum wealth threshold) on the day of sacrifice, making sacrifice wajib upon him.
الفقير (al-faqir): A person who does not own nisab; sacrifice is not wajib on him unless he purchases an animal with intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a poor person purchases a sound animal for sacrifice and then it becomes defective, what should he do?
A) He must buy another animal.
B) He may sacrifice the defective one and it suffices.
C) He must compensate for the loss.
D) He cannot sacrifice at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between wealthy and poor in cases of post-purchase defects based on the source of obligation: Shari'ah-based vs. purchase-specified.

Injury During Slaughter and the Role of Istihsan
Ruling on injury during slaughter with istihsan and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an animal that breaks its leg during slaughter, explain the istihsan basis, and identify the disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas: injury during slaughter preliminaries is legally attached to the slaughter itself.

This passage examines a practical sacrificial scenario: an animal breaks its leg while being positioned for slaughter. Does this defect invalidate the sacrifice? The Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to rule it valid, treating the injury as part of the slaughter process. This ruling contrasts with Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, who would consider the defect independently invalidating. The passage also includes an internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning delayed recapture.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
Identify the ruling: the sacrifice is valid (suffices) by istihsan. Note the key principle: the state of slaughter and its preliminaries (muqaddimat al-dhabh) are legally annexed (mulhaq) to the slaughter, so the injury is considered to have occurred during slaughter.
The term 'istihsan' is a distinct Hanafi source that allows departing from qiyas for a stronger consideration.
2
Identify the opposing position
Recognize that Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree. They would likely hold the sacrifice invalid because the animal must be free from defects at the moment of slaughter, and the broken leg is an independent defect.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul al-fiqh methodologies.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note the detail: if the animal escapes after being injured and is recaptured later, Imam Muhammad says the ruling remains valid even after a delay, while Abu Yusuf restricts validity to immediate recapture. The mufta bihi (operative position) is Imam Muhammad's view.
The disagreement hinges on whether the connection to the slaughter preliminaries is maintained after a break.
4
Apply the ruling to concrete scenarios
Work through the cases: (a) injury during laying down – valid; (b) injury before any slaughter action (e.g., during transport) – not attached, so invalid; (c) injury during laying down, escape, and recapture immediately – valid; (d) same but recapture after a delay – valid according to Muhammad, invalid according to Abu Yusuf.
The mufta bihi follows Muhammad, so the sacrifice is valid even after a delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اضجعها فاضطربت فانكسر رجلها فذبحها اجزاه استحسانا عندنا خلافا لزفر والشافعي رحمهما الله لان حالة الذبح ومقدماته ملحقة بالذبح فكانه حصل به اعتبارا وحكما وكذا لو تعيبت في هذه الحالة فانفلتت ثم اخذت من فوره وكذا بعد فوره عند محمد خلافا لابي يوسف لانه حصل بمقدمات الذبح
ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے جانور کو لٹایا پس وہ پھڑکا پس اس کا پیر ٹوٹ گیا پس اس نے اس کو ذبح کر دیا تو استحسانا ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے اختلاف ہے زفر اور شافعی کا اس لئے کہ ذبح کی حالت اور اس کے مقدمات ذبح کے ساتھ ملحق ہیں پس گویا کہ عیب ذبح کی وجہ سے حاصل ہوا۔
And if he laid it down and it struggled and its leg broke, then he slaughtered it, it suffices by istihsan according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, because the state of slaughter and its preliminaries are attached to slaughter, so it is as if it occurred [during slaughter] in consideration and ruling. And similarly, if it becomes defective in this state, and it escapes and then is taken immediately, and similarly after a while according to Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because it occurred due to the preliminaries of slaughter.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling by istihsan (juristic preference) regarding an injury that occurs during the slaughter process. The Hanafi school holds that if an animal struggles while being laid down for slaughter and breaks its leg, the sacrifice is still valid. This is because the state of slaughter and its preliminary actions (like laying the animal down) are considered part of the slaughter itself. By legal fiction, the injury is attributed to the slaughter process, so it does not affect validity. The ruling is contrary to Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i, who would consider the injury a defect that invalidates. An additional detail: if the animal escapes after being injured and is recaptured immediately, the ruling is the same; if recaptured after some time (after a delay), Imam Muhammad says it is still valid by istihsan, while Abu Yusuf disagrees because the connection to the slaughter preliminaries is broken. The relied-upon position is that of Imam Muhammad, as the basis is the attachment to the slaughter process.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases due to a stronger consideration.
مقدمات الذبح (muqaddimat al-dhabh): The preliminary actions of slaughter, such as laying the animal down and positioning it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a sacrificial animal breaks its leg while being laid down for slaughter, what is the ruling?
A) The sacrifice is invalid; another animal must be bought.
B) The sacrifice is valid by istihsan.
C) Valid only if the animal was recaptured immediately.
D) Valid only if the leg is not completely broken.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan allows the Hanafi school to overlook defects that occur during the slaughter preliminaries, treating them as part of the slaughter itself.

Defects After Purchase: Rich vs Poor and Guarantee
Ruling on defects appearing after purchase and guarantee for the poor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a defective sacrificial animal after purchase, explain the distinction between wealthy and poor, and apply the qiyas to zakat to negate liability (ḍamān) for the poor.

Core Idea
Wealthy must replace a defective sacrifice; poor may use it, with no compensation due.

When a sacrificial animal develops a defect after purchase, the obligation differs based on the buyer's wealth. This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies that a wealthy person must procure another animal, while a poor person may sacrifice the defective one. It further addresses whether the poor is liable for the loss in value, answering by analogy to zakat: just as destruction of part of the zakatable wealth does not create liability for the destroyed portion, the poor are not liable for the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between a wealthy person (māldār) and a poor person (faqīr) who purchases a sacrificial animal that later becomes defective.
Wealth is defined by possession of niṣāb; poverty by its absence.
2
State the ruling for each
For the wealthy, it is wājib to buy another animal. For the poor, it is permissible to sacrifice the defective one.
The ruling flows from the basis of obligation: wealth vs. intention at purchase.
3
Understand the reason (ʿillah) for the difference
Sacrifice becomes wājib on the wealthy due to their wealth, not the purchase. Hence the purchased animal is not designated (ghayr muʿayyan) for their obligation. For the poor, sacrifice becomes wājib precisely because of the purchase with intention, so the animal becomes designated (muʿayyan).
Designation (taʿyīn) is key: a designated animal fulfills the obligation even with defects.
4
Examine the qiyās to zakāt
The passage analogizes to a person who owes zakāt on 80,000 rupees, then 40,000 are destroyed before payment. He only pays zakāt on the remaining amount and is not liable for the destroyed portion.
The common principle: liability (ḍamān) does not arise when the obligation is contingent on the asset's existence.
5
Apply the analogy to the poor's defect
Similarly, the poor's obligation of sacrifice is contingent on the existence of the purchased animal. If a defect reduces its value, the poor is not liable for the deficiency; he simply sacrifices the animal as is.
This negation of ḍamān is the conclusion of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نہیں ہے جیسا کہ زکوۃ کے نصاب میں ۔ تشریح فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تفصیل اس وقت ہے جب کہ خریدنے کے وقت یہ عیوب موجود ہوں اور صحیح سالم خریدی اور پھر ایسا عیب پیدا ہو گیا کہ جس کی وجہ سے قربانی اس کی جائز نہیں رہی تو اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر وہ مالدار ہے تو اس پر دوسرا جانور خریدنا ضروری ہے۔ اور اگر فقیر ہے تو اسی کو قربان کر دے۔ اور فرق کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مالدار پر مالداری کی وجہ سے قربانی واجب ہوتی ہے نہ کہ خریدنے کی وجہ سے ، تو خرید کی ہوئی بکری مالدار کی قربانی کے لئے متعین نہیں ہے اور فقیر پر قربانی کی نیت سے خریدنے کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا خریدی ہوئی قربانی کے لئے متعین ہو جائے گی ۔ اس جانور میں جو نقصان ہو گیا ہے تو کیا فقیر پر کمی کا ضمان واجب ہوگا ؟ تو فرمایا کہ نہیں، جیسے مثلا کسی کے پاس اسی ہزار روپے تھے جن پر حولان حول کی وجہ سے زکوۃ واجب ہوگئی اور ابھی اس نے زکوۃ ادا نہیں کی تھی کہ ان میں سے چالیس ہزار ہلاک ہو گئے تو وہ صرف پانی کی زکو دے گا اور ہلاک شدہ کا ضامن نہیں ہو گا اسی طرح یہاں بھی ہے۔ مذکورہ قاعدہ
It is not like in the nisab of zakat. The commentary explains: This detail is when these defects are present at the time of purchase, and he purchased it sound and healthy, then such a defect appeared that due to which the sacrifice is no longer permissible. Its ruling is: if he is wealthy, then it is necessary for him to purchase another animal. And if he is poor, then he may sacrifice this one. And the reason for the difference is that sacrifice is obligatory upon the wealthy due to his wealth, not due to purchase, so the purchased goat is not specifically designated for the wealthy's sacrifice. And upon the poor, it becomes obligatory due to purchasing with the intention of sacrifice. Hence, the purchased animal becomes designated for sacrifice. Regarding the loss that occurred to this animal, is the poor liable for compensation for the deficiency? He said: No, just as, for example, someone had eighty thousand rupees upon which zakat became obligatory due to the passing of a year, and he had not yet paid the zakat, then forty thousand of them were destroyed. He will only give zakat on the remaining and is not liable for the destroyed amount. Similarly here. The mentioned rule.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi commentary on the ruling for a sacrificial animal that develops a defect after purchase. The key distinction is between a wealthy person (musir) and a poor person (faqir). For the wealthy, the obligation of sacrifice arises from their wealth (maldari), not from the purchase. Therefore, if the animal becomes defective, the wealthy must buy another to fulfill their wajib duty. For the poor, the obligation arises from the purchase itself made with the intention of sacrifice, so the animal becomes designated for sacrifice and may be sacrificed despite the defect. The passage then addresses whether the poor is liable for the decrease in value (daman) of the defective animal. By analogy to zakat—if zakat becomes due on wealth and part is destroyed, only zakat on the remainder is owed—the poor are not liable for the deficiency. This is based on the principle that the obligation for the poor is contingent on the animal's existence.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation or guarantee for a deficiency or loss.
مالدار (māldār): Wealthy person (musir) upon whom sacrifice is wajib due to possessing nisab.
فقیر (faqīr): Poor person who does not own nisab; for him, sacrifice is not wajib by wealth but becomes wajib upon purchasing with intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because he can afford another animal.
D) Because the seller must be compensated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling differentiates between wealthy and poor in defective sacrificial animals based on the basis of obligation, and absolves the poor from liability (ḍamān) by qiyās to zakāt.

Death or Loss of Sacrificial Animal: Ruling for Rich and Poor
Principle applied to death, loss, or theft of sacrificial animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the wealthy and poor when a sacrificial animal dies, is lost, or stolen, and apply the rule to scenarios where the animal is recovered during the days of sacrifice.

Core Idea
For the wealthy, obligation is tied to wealth; for the poor, to the act of purchase with intention.

This lesson applies the foundational principle that the obligation of sacrifice (wajib) varies with financial status to real-world contingencies: death, loss, or theft of the animal. The ruling clarifies whether a replacement is required and how to handle multiple animals when the original is found. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of sacrifice in unexpected situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text distinguishes between the wealthy (mūsar) and the poor (faqīr). Wealth is defined by possession of nisab (minimum threshold for sadaqah).
This categorization is key to all later rulings on sacrifice.
2
Apply the rule for death
If the purchased animal dies, the wealthy must buy another because their obligation is based on wealth. The poor need not replace it because their obligation arises only from the specific purchase.
Death does not create a new obligation for the poor.
3
Apply the rule for loss or theft
If the animal is lost or stolen and a replacement is bought, the replacement becomes a separate obligation. The original, if found, is still considered designated for sacrifice.
The act of buying anew creates a fresh obligation for both categories, but the effect differs.
4
Determine sacrifice when both animals are present
During the days of sacrifice (ayyam al-nahr), if both animals are available: the wealthy sacrifices only one (either the original or replacement) because only one is due; the poor must sacrifice both because each purchase obligated a sacrifice.
The wealthy's obligation is a single sacrifice; the poor's obligation multiplies with each purchase.
5
Explain the underlying rationale
The difference stems from the nature of obligation: for the wealthy, sacrifice is wajib from the start, so the specific animal is a means. For the poor, the purchase with intention creates a nazr (vow) that obligates the sacrifice of that particular animal.
Thus, loss and recovery affect the two categories differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن هذا الاصل قالوا اذا ماتت المشتراة للتضحية على الموسر مكانها اخرى ولا شئى على الفقير ولو ضلت او سرقت فاشترى اخرى ثم ظهرت الاولى فى ايام النحر على الموسر ذبح احدهما وعلى الفقير ذبحهما
And based on this principle, they said: If the animal purchased for sacrifice dies, then upon the wealthy [it is required to purchase] another in its place, and nothing is upon the poor. And if it is lost or stolen, then he bought another, then the first appears during the days of sacrifice, then upon the wealthy, he slaughters one of them, and upon the poor, he slaughters both.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage extends the earlier principle (Lesson 1) to cases of death, loss, or theft of the sacrificial animal. The rule remains consistent: for the wealthy, the obligation is tied to their wealth, not to the specific animal. Therefore, if the purchased animal dies, they must buy another. If the animal is lost or stolen and they buy a replacement, but then the original is found during the days of sacrifice, the wealthy need only slaughter one (either the original or the replacement). For the poor, whose obligation arises from the purchase with intention, both animals become designated for sacrifice; thus, if both are available, they must slaughter both. This is because each purchase created a separate obligation for the poor.

Key Terms
ایام النحر (ayyām al-naḥr): The days of sacrifice: 10th, 11th, and 12th of Dhul Hijjah.
موسر (mūsar): Wealthy person, one possessing nisab (minimum wealth threshold for sadaqah).
فقیر (faqīr): Poor person, one who does not possess nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Both
C) None, as the theft made it invalid
D) He may choose either

Key Takeaway
The obligation of sacrifice depends on financial status: wealthy must replace upon death, poor not; loss and recovery create multiple obligations for the poor but not for the wealthy.

Defect During Slaughter Handling: Ruling and Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity when defect occurs during slaughter handling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a sacrifice when a defect occurs during the preliminaries of slaughter, and distinguish the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding escape after the defect.

Core Idea
Preliminaries of slaughter are annexed to the slaughter itself by istihsan, so a defect arising from them does not invalidate.

When an animal breaks its leg while being laid down for slaughter, a strict analogy would invalidate the sacrifice. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to annex the preliminaries to the slaughter, preserving the validity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and explores an internal disagreement on escape and recapture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi ruling (jā'iz) for the case where the animal's leg breaks during laying down.
The ruling is based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.
2
Understand the Istihsan Reasoning
Analyze the key phrase 'annexed to the slaughter' (ملحقة بالذبح) and how it overrides qiyas.
The illa (effective cause) is the annexation (ilḥāq) of preliminaries to the slaughter itself.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Compare the positions of Imam Muhammad (valid if caught shortly after) and Abu Yusuf (valid only if caught immediately).
This difference arises from varying interpretations of the annexation's scope.
4
Compare with Opposing Schools
Contrast the Hanafi view with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who reject istihsan and rely on strict qiyas.
For them, the defect before slaughter invalidates the sacrifice.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Identify which intra-Hanafi position is generally relied upon (Imam Muhammad's) and why.
The text's 'kadhā' suggests it is akin to the relied view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اضجعها فاضطربت فانكسر رجلها فذبحها اجزاه استحسانا عندنا خلافا لزفر والشافعي رحمهما الله لان حالة الذبح ومقدماته ملحقة بالذبح فكانه حصل به اعتبارا وحكما وكذا لو تعيبت في هذه الحالة فانفلتت ثم اخذت من فوره وكذا بعد فوره عند محمد خلافا لابي يوسف لانه حصل بمقدمات الذبح
And if he lays it down, then it struggles, and its leg breaks, then he slaughters it, it suffices by istihsan in our school, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on them), because the state of slaughter and its preliminaries are annexed to the slaughter, so it is as if the defect occurred due to slaughter, in terms of legal consideration and ruling. Similarly, if it becomes defective in this state, then it escapes, then it is caught immediately, and likewise after an immediate [short delay] according to Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because it occurred during the preliminaries of slaughter.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where during the process of laying the animal down for slaughter, it struggles and breaks a leg. By istihsan (juristic preference), the sacrifice is valid, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i who would consider the defect as prohibiting. The reasoning is that the act of slaughter and its preliminaries (laying down, etc.) are legally annexed to the slaughter itself. Hence, any defect arising from these actions is considered as occurring at the time of slaughter and does not invalidate. The passage also addresses an extension: if the animal escapes after being laid and is caught immediately (fawran) or shortly after (ba'd fawran), the sacrifice is valid according to Imam Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf disagrees for the latter case. The point of contention is whether the escape and recapture are still part of the preliminaries.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling.
مقدمات الذبح (muqaddimāt al-dhabḥ): The preliminaries of slaughter, such as laying the animal down and sharpening the knife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an animal breaks its leg while being laid down for slaughter, what is the ruling?
A) The sacrifice is invalid.
B) The sacrifice is valid by istihsan.
C) It depends on whether the leg was broken intentionally.
D) The animal must be replaced.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, defects arising from the preliminaries of slaughter are legally annexed to the slaughter itself, so the sacrifice remains valid by istihsan.

Permissible Animals for Sacrifice and Age Requirements
Lists valid species and ages for sacrifice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of animals valid for sacrifice in the Hanafi school and identify the minimum age (thani or jadha') for each.

Core Idea
Only camels, cows, and sheep/goats are valid; each must be thani except sheep where a meaty jadha' suffices.

Sacrifice (udhiyah) is a wajib act for those who can afford it, but what animals are acceptable? This passage from al-Quduri's matn specifies that only three species are valid and sets minimum ages. Understanding these conditions ensures your sacrifice is legally valid and follows the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three permissible species
Read the first sentence: sacrifice is from camels, cows, and sheep/goats. Note that buffalo is included under cows.
Only these three types are accepted; other animals are not.
2
Define 'thani' for each species
A thani is a second-year animal: sheep/goats = 1 year, cows = 2 years, camels = 5 years.
The same word thani has different absolute ages per species.
3
Identify the exception for sheep
For sheep only, a jadha' (young sheep that has completed 6 months in the Hanafi view) suffices if it is meaty enough.
The hadith permits jadha' when thani is difficult.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Za'farani reported that jadha' is 7 months, but the madhhab of the fuqaha' is 6 months (mufta bihi).
The relied-upon position is 6 months.
5
Apply the ruling to buffalo and hybrids
Buffalo are like cows (same age rules). A hybrid offspring follows the mother's species for permissibility.
Example: if a wolf (wild) mates with a goat (domestic), the offspring is valid as a goat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والا ضحية من الابل والبقر والغنم لانها عرفت شرعا ولم تنقل التضحية بغيرها من النبي عليه السلام ولا من الصحابة رضى الله عنهم قال ويجزى من ذلك كله الثنى فصاعدا الا الضان فان الجذع منه يجزى لقوله عليه السلام ضحوا بالثنايا الا ان يعسر على احدكم فليذبح الجذع من الضان وقال عليه السلام نعمت الاضحية الجذع من الضان قالوا وهذا اذا كانت عظمية بحيث لو خلط بالثنيان يشتبه على الناظر من بعيد والجامع من الضان ما تمت له ستة اشهر فى مذهب الفقهاء وذكر الزعفرانى انه ابن سبعة اشهر والثنى منها ومن المعزا بن سنة ومن البقرا بن سنتين ومن الابل ابن خمس سنين ويدخل في البقر الجاموس لانه من جنسه والمولود بين الاهلى والوحشى يتبع الام لانها هي الاصل في التبعية حتى اذا نزا الذئب على الشاة يضحي بالولد
He said: And the sacrifice is from camels, cows, and sheep/goats, because they are known in Shari'ah and sacrifice with other than them has not been transmitted from the Prophet (peace be upon him) nor from the Companions. He said: And sufficient from all of that is the thani and above, except the sheep, for its jadha' suffices, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Sacrifice thaniyyahs, unless it is difficult for one of you, then let him slaughter a jadha' from sheep.' And he said (peace be upon him): 'Excellent is the jadha' from sheep as a sacrifice.' They said: And this is when it is meaty, such that if mixed with thaniyyahs it would be indistinguishable to an onlooker from a distance. And the jadha' from sheep is one that has completed six months in the madhhab of the jurists, and al-Za'farani mentioned that it is seven months. And the thani from it (sheep) and from goats is one year, from cows is two years, and from camels is five years. And buffalo enters under cows because it is of the same genus. And the offspring of a domestic and wild animal follows the mother because she is the origin in following, to the extent that if a wolf mounts a goat, the offspring may be sacrificed.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage states the types of animals valid for sacrifice (udhiyah) according to the Hanafi school, as found in the matn of al-Quduri. Only three species are allowed: camels, cows (including buffalo), and sheep or goats. This is because the practice of the Prophet and Companions was limited to these. For all these animals, a thani (second-year animal) is the minimum age, except for sheep (ḍān) where a jadha' (young one that has completed six months in the Hanafi view, or seven months according to al-Za'farani) is sufficient, based on hadith. The jadha' must be meaty enough to be confused with a thani from a distance. For goats, the must be one year old; for cows, two years; for camels, five years. The passage also clarifies that buffalo are considered a type of cow, so the same age rules apply. A hybrid offspring of a domestic and wild animal follows the mother for permissibility—e.g., if a wolf mates with a goat, the offspring is treated as a goat and can be sacrificed.

Key Terms
ثنی (thaniy): A second-year animal: from sheep/goats one year old, from cows two years, from camels five years.
جذع (jadha'): A young sheep that has completed six months (or seven according to some) and is meaty enough.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) One year (thani)
C) Two years
D) Five years

Key Takeaway
Sacrifice is only valid from camels, cows, and sheep/goats; each must meet the minimum age: thani for all except sheep where a meaty jadha' (≥6 months) suffices.

When an Escape During Slaughter Invalidates the Sacrifice
Explains ruling when sacrificial animal escapes during slaughter and recaptured

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's and Imam Abu Yusuf's positions on the validity of a sacrifice when a defective animal escapes during slaughter and is recaptured, and explain why the preliminaries of slaughter are annexed to slaughter.

Core Idea
A defect during preliminaries of slaughter does not invalidate sacrifice because those acts are annexed to slaughter itself.

In this passage, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. The issue: if a sacrificial animal that already has a defect escapes during slaughter but is quickly recaptured, does the sacrifice remain valid? The answer hinges on how we treat the preliminaries of slaughter. Let's unpack the reasoning and see how the ruling applies to both defective and non-defective animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses two related scenarios: (1) a defective sacrificial animal that escapes during slaughter and is recaptured immediately or after a short delay; (2) a non-defective animal that escapes and is recaptured immediately or after a short delay. Focus first on the defective case, which contains the dispute.
2
State the two positions
Imam Muhammad holds that the sacrifice is valid despite the defect, because the defect occurred during the preliminaries of slaughter (muqaddimat al-dhabh), which are annexed to slaughter. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the defect invalidates the sacrifice.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between the two major disciples of Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify the underlying principle
Imam Muhammad's argument rests on the principle that the preliminaries of slaughter (e.g., laying the animal down, starting the cut) are legally considered part of the slaughter. Therefore, a defect arising during those acts does not prevent the sacrifice from being valid, unless the defect would have prevented validity before slaughter began.
The commentator explicitly states: 'مقدمات ذبح ذبح کے ساتھ ملحق ہیں' (the preliminaries are annexed to slaughter).
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if a sacrificial animal has a defect (e.g., broken horn, limping) and escapes during the preliminaries but is recaptured quickly, Imam Muhammad allows the sacrifice, while Imam Abu Yusuf does not. The mufti must choose between these opinions.
The text does not explicitly state the mufta bihi, but the commentator's explanation supports Muhammad's view.
5
Apply the ruling to the non-defective case
For a non-defective animal that escapes after slaughter has begun (i.e., during preliminaries), if it is recaptured immediately or after a short delay, the sacrifice is valid by agreement. However, if it disappears and the owner intends to replace it, then catching it later does not suffice; the replacement animal must be sacrificed.
This second part is not disputed and provides an important contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر وہ عیب دار ہوا ہے اس حالت میں پھر وہ چھٹ کر بھاگا پھر اسی وقت اس کو پکڑ لیا گیا اور ایسے ہی فی الفور کے کچھ دیر بعد محمدؐ کے نزدیک بخلاف ابو یوسف کے اس لئے کہ وہ حاصل ہوا ہے مقدمات ذبح کے ساتھ۔ تشریح مقدمات ذبح ذبح کے ساتھ ملحق ہیں لہذا اگر ذبح کرتے وقت اس میں کچھ نقصان، اور فتور پیدا ہو گیا تو وہ عیب قربانی کے جواز کو روکنے والا نہ ہوگا۔ اگر قربانی کا جانور ذبح کے لئے لٹایا اور ذبح کرنا شروع کی اور چھٹ کر بھاگ گیا تو اگر فی الفور یا کچھ دیر بعد اس کو پکڑ لیا گیا تو اس کی قربانی جائز ہے البتہ اگر وہ غائب ہو گیا اور اس نے ارادہ کیا کہ دوسرا جانور ذبح کر دیں گے تو پھر احتیاط ملنے کے بعد بھی اس کو ذبح کرنا کافی نہ ہوگا۔
And if it (the animal) was defective in that state and then it escaped and was caught immediately, and similarly immediately after a short delay according to Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because it is achieved with the preliminaries of slaughter. Explanation: The preliminaries of slaughter are annexed to slaughter, so if during slaughter some defect or weakness occurred, that defect will not prevent the permissibility of the sacrifice. If the sacrificial animal is laid down for slaughter and slaughter is started, and it escapes and runs away, then if it is caught immediately or after a short delay, its sacrifice is permissible. However, if it disappeared and he intended to slaughter another animal, then even if he later finds it, slaughtering it will not be sufficient.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding a defective animal that escapes during slaughter. Imam Muhammad holds that if the animal was defective and then escaped but was recaptured immediately or after a short delay, the sacrifice is still valid because the defect occurred during the preliminaries of slaughter, which are annexed to the actual slaughter. Abu Yusuf disagrees and considers the defect as preventing validity. The commentator explains that the preliminaries (such as laying the animal down) are attached to slaughter, so a defect during those does not invalidate. Then a separate case: if a non-defective animal escapes after slaughter has begun, it is also valid if recaptured immediately or after a short delay. However, if it disappears and the owner intends to replace it, then recapturing it later does not suffice for the sacrifice.

Key Terms
مقدمات ذبح (muqaddimat dhabh): Preliminaries of slaughter, such as laying the animal down and starting the cut.
عيب ('ayb): A defect in the sacrificial animal that would normally prevent its validity.
فی الفور (fi al-fawr): Immediately, without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sacrifice is invalid because the animal was defective.
B) The sacrifice is valid because the defect occurred during the preliminaries of slaughter.
C) The sacrifice is only valid if recaptured immediately, not after a delay.
D) The sacrifice is invalid because the animal escaped.

Key Takeaway
The preliminaries of slaughter are annexed to slaughter itself, so a defect occurring during those preliminaries does not invalidate the sacrifice if the animal is recaptured promptly.
